    Ayuda

    Ayuda de edX

    Para preguntas sobre las clases, tareas o materiales del curso, utilice elforo de discusión.

    ¿Tiene preguntas generales sobre la plataforma edX? Puede encontrar mucha información valiosa en la sección de Preguntas Frecuentes de edX.

    ¿Tiene preguntas sobre algo más específico? Puede contactar directamente al equipo de soporte de la plataforma edX:

    Reportar un problema Hacer una sugerencia Formular una pregunta

    Nota: El equipo de soporte de edX tiene como idioma el inglés. Aunque haremos lo mejor posible para atender requerimientos en otros idiomas, nuestras respuestas serán siempre en inglés.

    Muchas gracias!

    Gracias por sus preguntas y aportes. Generalmente respondemos cada solicitud en un día hábil (Lunes a Viernes 13:00 UTC a 21:00 UTC.) Entre tanto, por favor revise nuestra sección de Preguntas Frecuentes detalladas en donde la mayoría de preguntas ya han sido respondidas.

    1. Contenidos , current location
    2. Updates & News
    3. Calendar
    4. Wiki
    5. Discusión
    6. Progreso
    cerrar

If you have code that calls a random function and then calls random.seed(0) or some other integer other than 0, subsequent times you run your program with the green run arrow button, the random function before you called random.seed(0) will now be seeded as well. To completely reset between runs, you will have to restart the kernel via the menu option (Run -&gt; Restart Kernel) or via the keyboard (Ctrl with the period key).</p> <p>Additionally, if you are writing a program and would like to generate pseudorandom number later on in the code, you can reset the seed to use the system clock with random.seed() (without any arguments).</p> <p><a     Drunken Tests - Time 8:22

    Cambiar a la versión navegable de la transcripción del video

    No se han encontrado los ficheros de video

    • 8:20 / 8:22
    Velocidad 1.0x
    1. 1.50x
    2. 1.25x
    3. 1.0x
    4. 0.75x
    Máximo Volumen.
    Llenar navegador
    Desactivar subtítulos
    Mover al final de la transcripción
    2. JOHN GUTTAG: I don't know about you guys out there at 600X land.
    3. But I confess I'm getting a little tired of the warm up and ready to
    4. build and run a simulation.
    5. So let's do that.
    6. If we look over here in the Idle window, we'll see that
    7. we've got three functions.
    8. They fit nicely on the screen.
    9. They will actually be sufficient to run the simulation.
    10. I want to go over each of them in a little bit of detail.
    11. And we'll do that over here on the PowerPoint side.
    12. So the first function is Walk.
    13. Not surprising, given what we're working with.
    14. It takes three arguments, a field, a drunk, and the number
    15. of steps to be walked.
    16. The first thing it does is it remembers the initial location of the
    17. drunk in the field by calling f.getLoc(d).
    18. It then runs through a simple For loop for s in range number of steps,
    19. f.moveDrunk(d).
    20. So moves the drunk one step each iteration of the loop in the field.
    21. And at the end, it returns the distance between the start and the
    22. final location of the drunk.
    23. The next function is simWalks.
    24. This will use Walk.
    25. It takes a number of steps and a number of trials.
    26. It begins by creating a drunk, for some reason named Homer.
    27. It then places the drunk at the origin, in this case, 0,0, initializes
    28. a variable that will be used to keep track of how far the drunk has moved
    29. in each of the trials.
    30. And then for t in range number of trials, it creates a new field, adds a
    31. drunk to the field, and then calls Walk, gets the number of steps, and
    32. appends it to Distances, which eventually it returns.
    33. You might be wondering for a minute where this came from.
    34. We hadn't talked about that before.
    35. Number of trials, which we're iterating over down here.
    36. The function simWalk calls Walk to simulate one walk.
    37. Because we're making a bunch of random choices during the walks, we don't
    38. want to infer too much about a typical walk from any one walk.
    39. After all, we could have one walk in which the drunk just oscillates back
    40. and forth across the origin.
    41. By running a large number of walks and then aggregating the results, we can
    42. get some inclination, some indication, of what a typical
    43. walk might look like.
    44. We'll do that in drunkTest.
    45. Number of trials.
    46. I've just arbitrarily here.
    47. I could have made it a parameter.
    48. But I've included the number of steps.
    49. So we'll take walks ranging from 10 steps to 100,000 steps.
    50. For each of those, we'll call simWalks to get a distance.
    51. And then we'll print three things, how far it was, that is to say how many
    52. steps were taken, the mean distance, because remember, we're running
    53. multiple trials, and the maximum and minimum distances to give us some
    54. insight, not only into what the average is, but what the variance is.
    55. All right.
    56. Let's go back over here to our idle window and actually run this.
    57. All right.
    58. We'll run drunkTest.
    59. Default number of trials is 20.
    60. That seems good enough.
    61. Let it rip.
    62. Now if we look at it, we let it rip.
    63. We got some distances.
    64. And interestingly, if we look at it, we see that indeed as our intuition
    65. had instructed us in the previous segment, the more steps, the further
    66. the drunk seems to be from the origin.
    67. Well at this point, we could walk away happy and say, OK, we
    68. have the right answer.
    69. Life is good.
    70. Well maybe.
    71. How do we know?
    72. How do we know there's not a bug in the step?
    73. In the simulation?
    74. Well let's go back and do something we talked about when we
    75. talked about debugging.
    76. Remember when we talked about debugging, we said it's always a good
    77. idea to run a test case when you know what the answer is and see if you get
    78. the expected answer.
    79. So let's do that here.
    80. I'm going to replace this line by numSteps 0 and 1.
    81. Because I have a pretty good intuition about how far the drunk should go in
    82. zero steps, that is to say, nowhere.
    83. And we saw before that when the drunk takes one steps, he should be moving
    84. exactly one step from the origin.
    85. Let's give this a try and see what happens.
    86. OK.
    87. It did give us what we expected.
    88. So probably we can feel pretty good about the result.
    89. Let's run--
    90. let's go back to our original version now.
    91. And I'm going to--
    92. well, let's just run it and see what we get again.
    93. All right.
    94. So I want you to notice here, is the first time we ran it,
    95. say with 10,000 steps.
    96. Did I say 100,000?
    97. Maybe that was what I had in the PowerPoint, but not what I have here.
    98. When we take 10,000 steps, the maximum was 248.
    99. Here, the maximum was 168.
    100. Should that make us nervous?
    101. Not at all.
    102. Remember, it is a random walk, and we do expect to have randomness.
    103. A trick worth knowing, however, is that sometimes we want to take the
    104. randomness out of a random walk or any random calculation.
    105. In particular, if we were to get the wrong answer, and we want to debug it,
    106. it's nice to get reproducible results.
    107. We can do that by using something called Random.seed.
    108. So let's come over here and type Random.seed of well,
    109. any number will do.
    110. But I'm going to use 0.
    111. Let's go back for a moment to the PowerPoint now, where I'm going to try
    112. and show you what's going on.
    113. We're looking here at what happens with Random.seed.
    114. We had talked about Random.choice generating a
    115. sequence of random choices.
    116. In fact, what it generates is a sequence of pseudo-random choices.
    117. For our purposes, we don't need to worry too much about the difference
    118. between pseudo-random and random.
    119. It will give us the same kind of results a true random number generator
    120. would give us.
    121. But here's the way it works.
    122. When we first started, it generates a sequence of random numbers.
    123. Then the first time we need one, it returns the first number.
    124. And it returns the second.
    125. Then it returns the third, et cetera.
    126. These sequences are generated using something called the Seed.
    127. Depending upon the seed, we get a pseudo-random sequence, a sequence of
    128. unpredictable numbers.
    129. Typically the seed is chosen using the system clock.
    130. For example, the number of milliseconds since 1978.
    131. Since we don't know when the program will be run, that will give us a
    132. random result.
    133. However, if I write random.seed(0), or 0 or 2 or anything else I care to
    134. write, it says use this seed.
    135. And then I can use that seed over and over again, and then each test I will
    136. get the same random sequence.
    137. And we'll get the same things.
    138. So let's try it over here with drunkTest again.
    139. I've run it once.
    140. Now I'm going to run it--
    141. I've got a different result from the last one.
    142. But now we should get the same result because we're using the same seed.
    143. And sure enough, if you compare these two sets of results, you'll see that
    144. they are indeed identical.
    145. A good trick to keep in mind when you're debugging your programs.

    Video errata

    At 2:14 the screen shows the code for simWalks. The second to last line says:
    'distances.append(walk(f, homer, numTrials))'
    The line should be:
    'distances.append(walk(f, homer, numSteps))'

    source code and slides from this lesson

    Canopy Users: If you have code that calls a random function and then calls random.seed(0) or some other integer other than 0, subsequent times you run your program with the green run arrow button, the random function before you called random.seed(0) will now be seeded as well. To completely reset between runs, you will have to restart the kernel via the menu option (Run -> Restart Kernel) or via the keyboard (Ctrl with the period key).

    Additionally, if you are writing a program and would like to generate pseudorandom number later on in the code, you can reset the seed to use the system clock with random.seed() (without any arguments).

Los temas incluyen la biología, negocios, química, ciencias de la computación, economía, finanzas, electrónica, ingeniería, alimentación y nutrición, historia, humanidades, derecho, literatura, matemáticas, medicina, música, filosofía, física, ciencia, estadísticas y mucho más. EdX es una iniciativa en línea sin fines de lucro creado por los socios fundadores Harvard y MIT.
